B009ERK1T0|5.0|As many other other reviewers out there on the net have pointed out, 'Koi No Yokan' is a Japanese phrase that describes the sense one gets when they know they will fall in love with someone. It's a beautiful concept and one that, when paired to what Deftones deliver sonically, gives the listener a direction for where the music is intended to take them internally: higher planes. Deftones don't want you to remain here on earth, they want to lift you up to that place where existential experience is beautiful, meaningful. It's debateable whether this 'phase' intended to begin with White Pony, but it certainly reached its peak with Diamond Eyes, in itself a dynamically powerful record, a perfect record. Koi No Yokan is, for me, on par with Diamond Eyes. It is that good.<br /><br />Deftones were classed as nu-metal when Adrenaline came out but they have never been a band to give into the angst and rage of their contemporaries. Tool decimated religious orders, and Rage Against the Machine decimated political orders, but Deftones just existed creating riffs and sounds that made people stop and tilt their heads. They gradually changed approaches, intending to rise above petty emotionalism and take you on an auditory journey. Originally that started with headbanging but somewhere along the line they realised people might want to hold up a candle instead.<br /><br />With Koi No Yokan, Deftones work to build atmosphere, which has become an essential part of any Deftones album. When Chino and the rest talk about 'dynamics', you hear it in play with tracks that are built to create an album as a whole. Carpenter and co create moshworthy riffage, responding to Chino's crooning, (and leopard screams) - his voice is a true instrument in itself. But none of the album is distasteful, it's all deliciously 'accessible' for new listeners. There are rest sections, hard sections, beautiful sections. Where Deftones excel are replacing aforementioned rage and angst with another emotion - love. Love is a strong theme for this band, whether it's love for a downed bandmate, for the experience of highs and lows of life, or for a lover. Love can be beautiful, powerful, but also heavy. To my knowledge, they are the only metal band that does this as successfully as they do.<br /><br />Brief breakdown of songs (note that my Australian release came with Swerve City as the first song):<br />1. Swerve City - Punchy syncopated guitar punches introduce you to Koi No Yokan. It's typical Deftones fair with some new dynamic to Chino's approach to vocals and some spacy atmospheric work in the latter half of the song. 4/5.<br />2. Romantic Dreams - A schizophrenic song with a few different colours to show. What begins as one those painfully urgent emotional sounding Deftones tracks kicksteps back and forth between reflection and aggression, then once again with a simply and catchy chorus. One of my favourite tracks. 5/5.<br />3. Leathers - Another spacey introduction hides a big fat right hook. Perhaps this should have been the opening track, it would have suited it. A heavy track, but with a beautiful primary chorus followed by a vocal enhanced secondary chorus. One of the first released to the public. Like something from Diamond Eyes. 5/5.<br />4. Poltergeist - This is perhaps the most straightforward track on the album, bringing nothing new to fans but it sounds like it belongs as one of the better songs off their self-titled albums. I like the lyrics personally and it carries a bit of energy and is more speedy than others on the album. 4/5.<br />5. Entombed - Perhaps the 'Digital Bath' or 'Change' of Koi No Yokan, Entombed is one of the softer, slower efforts. A pretty song, the band really asks you to be gentle with them here. 4/5.<br />6. Graphic Nature - Another classic harder Deftones song, with some great jazz-styled drumwork. Another song where Chino delves into someones mind. The chorus is awesome, perhaps one of the better songs. 5/5.<br />7. Tempest - This has to be one of Deftones best songs to date. There's not much to say as it was one of their first-released to the public along with Leathers. Catchy riffage, absolutely beautiful vocal work and a perfect blend of soft and hard in the one song. 5/5.<br />8. Gauze - Another classic Deftones rocker, and reminds me of something they might have done from Saturday Night Wrist. A churning and angry lead in with a beautiful chorus. A high energy track that heightens the listening experience. 4/5.<br />9. Rosemary - The softest and longest track here with a beautifully slow lead in, this song sucks you right in. A really spacey and pretty song that's perhaps the embodiment of the 'Koi No Yokan' ideal. The 'Passenger' of today. 5/5.<br />10. Goon Squad - Don't let the name fool you, the song is fun, aggressive and energetic, perhaps a little bit like 'CMND/CTRL' but with greater dynamics. It's surprising to see songs plastered at the bottom of the tracklist that could fit anywhere on the tracklist order realistically. Typical Deftones, a moshpit-pleaser. 5/5.<br />11. What Happened to You - A newer sounding dynamic for the Deftones. Another beautiful song with a newer sound we haven't really heard from them before - more atmosphere and an almost pop feel to it. Not a bad thing here, a nice closer! 4/5.<br /><br />I would not say this is their 'opus' or a 'masterpiece', but it's CLOSE. As you can see, there is no bad song here. Perhaps we will consider it a masterpiece in 10 years time. But, I would say it rates on par (if not better) with Diamond Eyes as their strongest overall effort. For Deftones fans, this is truly a treat. Don't just listen. Feel.

His disappearance gets the attention of the CIA due to their fear that, if the secrets in this one agent's head were revealed, many agents in the field would be compromised.  The whole thing seems fishy to Rapp from the start, and as he continues his investigation, he is targeted by an assassin and gets in the middle of an intragovernmental mess.  But true to what we know of Rapp, he overcomes injuries and setbacks and doggedly pursues truth and justice.<br /><br />Flynn doesn't disappoint with his detailed fighting action, believable political intrigue, and plot twists to keep the reader guessing.  I can relate to Rapp's intolerance for terrorist coddling, and his frustration with bureaucratic garbage.  Sure, he makes rash decisions, but he's always right in the end.<br /><br />Fans of Mitch Rapp will be delighted by The Last Man.  Readers who have never read Flynn's books will love it and will want to go back to the beginning to read them all.  All will wish and hope that the USA has someone like Rapp to call on when we need him.<br /><br />Thanks to Edelweiss and the publisher for the complimentary electronic review copy!

B0041KVW2K|3.0|Re-doing my review as I now have a full US boxed set (not from Amazon - I actually purchased from Walmart.com).  I have listened to most of the albums now, and can give a more detailed SQ review.<br /><br />Cosmetics:  It's a very nice looking set.  A flip-up box (be careful taking the slip cover off, however), and the LPs are all sealed in plastic and look to be the same as the individual LPs being sold, sans the big sticker.  Packaging is imo acceptable - not the 60's thick stock paper, but not the 1980's flimsy stock paper either.  Basically, if you've bought a modern vinyl LP, it's very similar packaging.  The jackets are replicas of the UK pressings, for the most part.  And the vinyl is 180g and also replicates the UK labels from the 1960's.  The book is quite nice, heavy, and well made.  Overall a very handsome set.<br /><br />Sound quality:  I'll break this up into two sections.  First and foremost is \\"how do these sound?\\".  I'm very pleased with what the Abbey Road engineers have done, with what they had.  From a technical standpoint, it's been said that these are 24/44.1k digital source - very similar to the USB stick but with no limiting (means less LOUD).  I've compared most of these LP's with 1987 CD's, a BC13 (Blue Box), and 2009 CD's.  Imo these LP's are significantly better than the CD's.  Comparing them with the 2009 CD's specifically, they are less loud, more engaging, less cold and clinical.  This obviously depends on your turntable setup, of course.<br /><br />Comparing it with a BC13 is harder.  The BC13 generally is brighter (higher treble), but also has weaker bass.  For the remasters, it's generally the reverse.  So they sound different but generally neither one beats the other imo.  If you have a BC13, these new LP's won't replace them, they just compliment them.<br /><br />The big issue I have right now, and the reason for three stars, is the problems I'm having with the actual vinyl pressings themselves.  More than half of my LPs have \\"non-fill\\" issues - it's a pressing defect that shows up visually as a stitching pattern, or \\"a string of pearls\\", in bright light.  Audibly, you hear it as a ripping sound in one channel, almost like your stylus is going over a brush.  Some of these defects are barely audible, but on some songs it's very distracting.  For example, on my copy of Revolver, \\"Here There and Everywhere\\" has quite a few non-fill defects and it basically ruins that particular song.<br /><br />Again, I did not buy it at Amazon, but so far it sounds like US pressings in general are affected by this - I guess as time goes on and more reviews are put up, we might know more.<br /><br />12/2/2012 update:  After doing a deep dive on all my LP's, I ended up with 8 LP's with issues - 7 being non-fill (that tearing sound in the left channel) and one being heavily noisy, even after a wet cleaning.  Of the 7 with non-fill, I can live with 2 of them as they are minor / in places not very noticeable.  But the other 5 are imo unacceptable, so overall 6 LP's that I need to somehow exchange or else I need to return my boxed set.fill\\" issues - it's a pressing defect that shows up visually as a stitching pattern, or \\"a string of pearls\\", in bright light. B009NOVCTM|4.0|[This review is of the standard three disc (50 tracks) edition]<br /><br />Fifty years after forming, the Rolling Stones release 'GRRR!,' a career retrospective that, imo, is far superior to their 40th anniversary release, 'Forty Licks,' though it's debatable whether it's really essential for owners of that set, even with the two new tracks and slightly better sound. Like that previous collection, it serves as both a great introduction to the band for newcomers, and as a nice compilation of their most essential tracks for the casual fan. But, even more so than that last set, it also serves as a remarkable historical document for the more serious fan, showing the evolution of one of rock's most important bands.<br /><br />The key advantage that this collection holds over 'Forty Licks'--besides the fact that it's ten tracks longer--is that the track sequencing is in chronological order (though the Summer of Love-era \\"She's a Rainbow\\" is placed with the early 70's tracks for some reason), and shows just how far the band had come in just a few short years, from a charismatic R&B covers act with an attitude to one of the biggest and most influential bands in the world. There are 24 tracks from the 60's, twelve from the 70's, seven from the 80's, three from the 90's, and four from the 21st century--two being brand new--providing a pretty comprehensive overview of their career.<br /><br />The sound quality on 'GRRR!' is stellar, with none of the clipping (distortion, basically) common to a lot of modern day reissues of older material, as it appears this collection uses the same versions as were on the excellently remastered albums released over the past decade or so (though edited in some cases)*. The songs do seem slightly louder than on their remastered studio album counterparts and 'Forty Licks,' but the dynamic range is not affected as far as I can tell. The quiet parts are still quiet, and the loud parts are still loud, thankfully.<br /><br />The two new tracks, \\"Doomand Gloom\\" and \\"One More Shot,\\" didn't do much for me initially, but the patented Stonesy sleazy swagger of both tracks is beginning to grow on me, as some of the band's best material have had to do in my case over the years (including almost all of 'Exile,' an undeniable masterpiece...not that I'm comparing). Within seconds of playing each song, there's no mistaking just who's performing them.<br /><br />The one major negative about this set is that, like 'Forty Licks,' many of the tracks here are in edited form. I would have preferred to pay a few extra bucks for an extra disc if it meant getting the full versions, but considering this is a collection of their hits, which are usually presented in edited form on the radio anyway, this shouldn't be a huge deal to the casual fan, who are probably the main target of this release.<br /><br />Even though hardcore fans will own most of these songs already, it's still awesome to hear--with pristine sound--the progression of this inimitable band over the years, so I definitely recommend 'GRRR!' for both them and for those who just want the band's essential tracks in one place. It's amazing to think that the Stones are still going strong after 50 years, with no signs of letup in sight. To me, they are the perfect \\"anytime\\" band. Morning, noon, night, rain, snow, whether you're happy or sad, it's the perfect time for some Stones.<br /><br />Though they will probably always be known as the second greatest band of rock's golden age, no band defined--and still define--rock and roll like the Stones. They exude--ARE--rock and roll. And there's no better evidence than this collection.<br /><br />(*Edit--Someone in the comments section brought to my attention that the more recent remasters, such as 'Some Girls' and 'Sticky Fingers,' do contain clipping. I've compared many of the mid-late 60's tracks here with their 2002 remastered counterparts, and they appear the same to me, with no clipping. I'm not hearing any clipping on the 'Some Girls' and 'Sticky Fingers' tracks here either.)

B009AMAMF2|4.0|How do you even begin to write a review for Cloud Atlas? I saw it two days ago, and woke up at 4:15 this morning, thinking about how to organize my thoughts into a coherent assessment.<br /><br />I will probably fail.<br /><br />If I were to attempt to sum up the movie in a single paragraph, I would say that it deals with how souls change over time. We are shown six distinct time periods. Several actors appear as six different characters across these six moments in time. Oh, the time periods all feature different genres too. I would imagine the movie is attempting to make us question the meaning of life itself, and how it might be represented on some sort of eternal scale. It's like watching the offspring of [[ASIN:B00005JKJA Mulholland Dr.]] and [[ASIN:B004FVHBXY Inception [Blu-ray]]] playing on a holodeck, featuring six worlds across 500 years, using a [[ASIN:B004FHCH96 Memento (10th Anniversary Special Edition) [Blu-ray]]]-style narrative.<br /><br />In order to make sense of the movie and give it an accurate grade, I would have to read the book (unlikely), and watch it at least one more time (very likely when the Blu-ray is released). For now, I'll document my thoughts to see whether subsequent viewings change my opinion.<br /><br />The movie is based on the book by David Mitchell, who reportedly wrote stories for the six different time periods, utilizing six different genres, and then drew them together as the story progressed. That structure reminds me of Dan Simmons' [[ASIN:B001VFB5RA The Hyperion Cantos]], although I doubt it matches Hyperion in terms of quality. I highly recommend Hyperion to anyone who appreciates Cloud Atlas. In terms of subject matter, I'm reminded of the [[ASIN:0553565214 Daggerspell (Deverry Series, Book One)]] series, written by Katharine Kerr. It also jumps around in time, across centuries, and uses the theme of souls being continually drawn to one another. I wonder whether Mitchell is a fan?<br /><br />The six time periods used in the movie are 1849, 1936, 1973, 2012, 2144, and the twenty-fourth century.<br /><br />Through these six stories, we learn that the souls of some of the characters involved also travel through time. However, they may change from male to female, and change race, or sexual orientation. Some souls are evil in every setting, while others switch from evil, or neutral, to good. I won't reveal any of that information because it's better to discover it for yourself.<br /><br />The biggest problem with the movie is the way the story keeps switching from one time period to another. Instead of showing each story arc from beginning to end, we are often shown two or three minutes in one time period before jumping to another. This has the effect of making it clear that the characters (or souls) exist in several or (in some cases) all of the time periods, and that's a good thing. But it also prevented me from becoming too emotionally attached to any of the characters. I found that I didn't really care about their struggles.<br /><br />The earliest story takes place on a ship (South Pacific), and moves through a hotel in the 1930s (Scotland), to San Francisco in 1973, a party set in an English hotel in the present day, before visiting the future (Neo Seoul) and far future. So you'll see two period pieces, a sequence that reminded me of [[ASIN:B000LV6VZQ The Streets of San Francisco - Season One, Vol. 1]], a story set in our current time, a science fiction segment that looks like [[ASIN:B00845MRKE Blade Runner 30th Anniversary Collector's Edition (4-Disc Blu-ray / DVD +Book +UltraViolet Digital Copy Combo Pack)]], [[ASIN:B000OPPBEQ The Ultimate Matrix Collection [Blu-ray]]] and [[ASIN:B000QTD368 The Fifth Element (Remastered) [Blu-ray]]], and a post-apocalyptic tale set more than 300 years in the future.<br /><br />Technology ranges from primitive to futuristic, with the final segment being a curious blend of both.<br /><br />The strongest of the stories are the final three chronologically. In 2012, Timothy Cavendish (Jim Broadbent) is a book publisher who desperately needs money. This segment features a lot of humor and had the audience laughing throughout. The Neo Seoul story features Sonmi-451 (Doona Bae), who is aided in her quest by Hae-Joo Chang (Jim Sturgess). Here we see plenty of futuristic shots and an elaborate chase scene which would fit well in any science fiction movie. The final segment sees Zachry (Tom Hanks) meet Meronym (Halle Berry). Although the two are from vastly different backgrounds, they form a bond. It's here that the biggest struggle between good and evil takes place, and it shows how Zachry's soul evolves from what it once was.<br /><br />None of the stories are straightforward. Most are not properly resolved. I think the point is not the quests themselves, but the overall passage of the souls of the recurring characters.<br /><br />I'm probably not making much sense.<br /><br />Cloud Atlas is something you need to experience for yourself, but only if you are the kind of person open to analysis and repeat viewings. This is not a coherent story with a linear structure. If you do decide it's for you, try to enjoy the individual scenes for what they are. If you are anything like me, you'll reflect on what you have seen over the next few days and things will start to take on some kind of meaning. It's certainly one of the most ambitious cinematic undertakings I have ever witnessed.<br /><br />Overall provisional score 3.5/5cally. 0316223239|3.0|The book that I am reviewing is Room by Emma Donoghue.  The story is about a 5 year-old boy named Jack who has lived in a room with his mother since he was born.  Jack and his mother are being held against their free will, but to Jack this life seems normal because it is the only life that he knows.  Jack's mom is determined to keep her son happy.  She does what ever she can to teach him to read and keep him safe so he will learn and grow.  To Jack he is fine in this room because he is with his mother who he loves very much.  Overall, I feel that the book is thoughtful and creative and sends various messages throughout the story.  It is very interesting for me to see the way we live in a different light.  For example, to Jack, weekly supplies to his room on Sundays are exciting and he doesn't understand the reason for these deliveries.  He also doesn't realize the torture that his mom has to endure.  This is also a great story because you can see how close a mother and son can be. The thing that I liked best about the book is that the story was unpredictable.  For this reason I couldn't stop reading.  It can definitely be said that Emma Donoghue knows how to grab the reader into her book.  I found it interesting that the idea of the story has a scary concept, but since Jack tells the story, it doesn't seem scary to me at all.  There are many things that I like about the book, but on the other hand, there are some things I would have done differently if I were the author.  One thing that I was not fond of is how the book is laid out.  I feel that the whole first half of the story could have been condensed.  To me the majority of the first part of the book seems to be just descriptive details of the characters and how they live rather than an actual plot.  At the half waypoint of the book I was getting a little bored.  It seemed at this point that the main problem in the book had already been resolved, however, as you read on, you do see a reason for the second half of the story.  Life inside the room is not easy for Jack and his mom, but neither is life outside of the room.  The second half of the book allows the reader to sympathize with Jack and his mom just as much as you do in the first part.  As for the target audience of the book, I feel that the book is a great read for many different kinds of people.  However, it is definitely not the best book for younger children to read.  Not so much in the sense of bad language, but more for the reason that there are some ideas that only maturity can help one understand.  I feel that this is a good book for a reader that is in high school or older.  I also think that moms could really relate to the relationship between Jack and his mother.  Overall I really enjoyed reading Room.

1451620829|4.0|I read a lot of rock autobiographies (MÃ¶tley CrÃ¼e, Pete Townsend, Keith Richards), and I always approach them with a contradictory mindset:  on the one hand, I assume that the stories are at least somewhat exaggerated in order to enhance their star-power appeal...but on the other hand, I feel like I have to take them at more-or-less face value, because otherwise, what's the point of reading them at all?  Given that, and my lifelong devotion to all things Kiss, I found Peter's book to be at times astonishing, entertaining, hyper-emotional, and just plain raw.<br /><br />To date, Peter's former bandmates Paul Stanley and (especially) Gene Simmons have had the biggest microphone when it comes to tailoring the \\"truth\\" in Kisstory.  They did all the work, Peter and (lead guitarist) Ace Frehley were lazy and drugged out, etc. etc.  Ace's memoirs (\\"No Regrets\\") helped tell his side of the story, but many fans were disappointed when he didn't dish a little MORE dirt in those pages.  Well, fear not:  in \\"Makeup To Breakup,\\" Peter serves up heaping platters of dirt, sometimes to the extent that he comes across as moving beyond a \\"bitchy\\" category into full-blown nastiness.  The thing is...he's also incredibly hard on himself, and for every instance that he manages to smear Paul and Gene (and Ace, as you read on), he also takes a lot of - credit? - for the ways he HIMSELF screwed up.<br /><br />Peter's drug addiction was hidden from fans for decades, but with the cards laid on the table for him (thanks, Gene!), here he admits exactly how MUCH drugging he was doing, and it's really astonishing:  mountains of cocaine, by his own admittance 5 grams a day, and not just him, but basically EVERYONE in the Kiss Kamp BUT Paul and Gene.  Manager Bill Aucoin, his lover Sean Delaney, all the crew, producer Bob Ezrin...EVERYONE was doing blow.  His wives (both Lydia and Deb), his friend John Belushi...it was constant.  Does Peter take the blame for this?  He does.  But as he does, he also paints a really accurate picture of how inescapable and \\"normal\\" it all was.  For me, the most telling point Peter made was that the four guys in the group were necessary to keep it all going: all the money, the drugs, the lifestyle...so that, if you wanted drugs, you placed an order with someone on the crew, and a whole bag was just delivered to your door:  coke, pills, booze, you name it.  Want women?  Here's a suite in the hotel with 50 of them, just come on in and take 2 or 3 at a time, then go back later for more.  All the hangers-on, from the manager on down, relied on the band for their own lifestyles, and so had to keep the members happy.  One of the interesting insights Peter makes about this extravagance is that really EVERYONE in Kiss was an addict...it just wasn't always drugs.  With Peter, it was, and for Ace, it was booze.  Gene was addicted too, though:  to sex and power.  Gene has always maintained that he voraciously consumed women, but Peter makes it real in a way Gene hasn't so far.  And Paul?  To read Peter tell it, Paul was basically addicted to...himself.  His narcissism was profound, and he used that to exercise power within the band, and the broader company that Kiss became.<br /><br />Peter occasionally lapses into some rather wincing crudity when he really lets go:  witness his referral to (current Kiss guitarist) Tommy Thayer as \\"Paul and Gene's butt boy.\\"  Ouch...that's just crass.  But he's had a long time to build up some venom, and until now, his story has only really been told in Gene's books, ex-wife Lydia's book, and interviews with Gene and Paul.  Now, he gets his say, and he holds back no punches.  The result is a whirlwind of emotion, faith, regret, and vitriol that helps balance the scales in terms of the way Kisstory is/will be written.  This isn't a book written by a current \\"rock god\\" who sees himself as bigger and badder than everyone else; it's a book written by a 67-year old man who cringes at some of his ridiculous behavior (he and Paul, naked at Gene's door, serving their balls in champagne glasses to Gene's groupie), but who doesn't hold back on CALLING himself ridiculous, and that's incredibly refreshing.  For me, this is the best of the Kiss memoirs thus far; I'm interested to see how Paul remembers these events when he finally gets around to doing HIS book.
B008G59IKO|5.0|&iexcl;UNO! delivered a more classic Warning/Nimrod era Green Day sound and I wasn't exactly sure what to expect with the arrival of &iexcl;DOS! six weeks later. I didn't have much to go on other than the fact that the band called the album a \\"Party Album\\" and a \\"Dirty Rock\\" record. So what exactly is &iexcl;DOS!?<br /><br />First off, this record is very different from &iexcl;UNO! in that it has a ton of energy. Most of the songs pack a punch and the record as a whole is almost unrelenting in terms of pace. This isn't the same punk energy that you would have found on the early Green Day albums. This is something different and clearly inspired by early 70's rock. The first comparison that comes to mind for me would be The Rolling Stones \\"Exile on Main St\\", but there are about 200 albums from that era you could list that Green Day could have taken inspiration from here.<br /><br />Lyrically this could not contrast the serious political messages in American Idiot and 21st Century Breakdown more. Although the content is just as real, it is different because the focus is more internal and self glorifying topics. From \\"F*** Time\\" to \\"Makeout Party\\", the guys were clearly just trying to have fun on this and not taking themselves or the lyrical tone too seriously. This record seems to be an authentic reflection of the rock star element of their lifestyle, for better or worse. The lyrics provide a window into Billie Joe's excessive lifestyle and mindset during the last few years which can be really interesting. Part of me is glad to see them let loose here and just write songs they enjoy and not care about repercussions, but some listeners on a moral high horse may be turned off by the lyrics.<br /><br />So how's the music? Overall it's catchy, and well written. There are definitely just as many fantastic songs on here as on &iexcl;UNO!. That said, it's really hard to compare the two because these tracks are blisteringly fast reinterpretations of 70's singalong rock songs--not pop punk. The tracks \\"Lazy Bones\\", \\"Baby Eyes\\", and \\"Ashley\\" are probably the best constructed songs, and there should be no surprise that these tracks also sound the most like classic Green Day tracks. Another highlight for me is \\"Wow! That's Loud\\" which has a chorus on it that will make any fan of The Who squeal with delight. The only two songs which break from this aforementioned fast pace are \\"Nightlife\\", which shows Green Day experimenting collaborating with a female rapper, and \\"Amy\\", which is a gorgeous acoustic track about the late Amy Winehouse.<br /><br />Overall I have to say I really enjoy this record. I feel the casual Green Day fan may have a negative reaction to this album because there's no \\"singles\\" and they probably never heard the Foxboro Hot Tubs project, or have a great appreciation for late 1970' rock. Selling over 60 million albums worldwide, Green Day has become a household music brand name at this point. This brand name correlates with a certain amount of refinement and production that this record simply does not have, and that lack of polish is the exact point of this record!<br /><br />As a rock music junky I absolutely love this record, but for marketing purposes this record maybe should been a second Foxboro Hot Tubs album. I say that because that's really the context you should listen to this album in. If you haven't heard that Green Day side project, grab it now. Grab it BEFORE you listen to &iexcl;DOS!. 1451696191|4.0|(Originally on my blog, The Reader's Commute)<br /><br />For those of you who are late to the party like me, and haven't had the chance to read the book (or see the delightful movie - more about that later), Wallflower is an epistolary novel by Stephen Chbosky.  The book is told in letters, written by the narrator, Charlie, to an unnamed \\"friend.\\"  The story is set in the early 90s (which is something I totally dig, as can be seen in my last review for The Adults), where people still wrote letters and received mix-tapes  and had to (gasp) talk on home telephone lines!  Charlie is beginning his freshman year of high school, and he's very nervous about it.  Aside from losing his best friend the year before, Charlie deals with a lot of internal emotional trauma, which certainly effects his narrative voice.<br /><br />This was what was most shocking to me: the voice.  I had always assumed, from what people had told me about the book, that I would be reading the voice of a nerdy, shy teenager.  Instead, I was reading a sometimes-endearing, but also terribly-heartbreaking account of a troubled boy.  Sure, I understood Charlie's formative experiences and adored the beautiful quotes that are so often quoted on people's Facebook pages, like:<br /><br />  \\"So, I guess we are who we are for alot of reasons. And maybe we'll never know most of them. But even if we don't have the power to choose where we come from, we can still choose where we go from there. We can still do things. And we can try to feel okay about them.\\"<br /><br />I loved these moments.  I appreciated them.  But this book had a level of intimacy that made me uncomfortable in a way.  Believe me, I've read subject matter that has been much worse and much more explicit than this, but it was the innocence of this narrator that really tripped me up.<br /><br />I was reminded of an exercise that I did in my memoir writing class, where I had to stare another person in the eyes for sixty seconds.  It was an uncomfortable task, because I'm not used to doing that (who is?).  It was alarming, allowing someone to look me in the eyes for that long - it's almost like I felt defenseless.  However, I also felt incredibly connected in that moment, realizing I'm existing with that other person.  So maybe that sums up how I felt about Charlie and his narration.<br /><br />I read this book in a single afternoon.  When I finished reading, I kind of curled up into a ball on my bed and felt weird.  I imagine this sounds like I didn't like the book; really, I think it takes a very good book to make you feel that way.
B0041KVW2K|2.0|First off I am rating the quality of the set here.  We already know the Beatles are 5 star all the way.<br />These albums are cut from 24 bit low rate sampled masters and do not have the superior stereo image of the original fully analog pressings.  Recommend just getting the CDs instead.  The sound character of the albums is what I would call neutral with balanced frequency response.  On some material Bass particularly is more realistic. The new mastering is similar to the CD Box set though with less compression  and I like it. So that part is good.  I agree that the overall sound is good when compared to other vinyl that comes from low sample rate digital masters but it could have been so much better. The bad thing is that on a good stereo system these pressings do not sound as good stereo image wise as what fully analog Beatles pressings do.  NOWHERE near it.  The image is flat with no spatial characteristics whatsoever.  On a cymbal the effects are especially pronounced. That is to be expected from digital 24 bit/44.1 Khz sampled material.  The WOW factor is just not there.  There is NO way these are from 192/24 masters and Abbey admits it.<br />Mobile Fidelity vinyl sourced from analog masters BLOWS them away with superior stereo image and Mobile Fidelity is not known as the best sound quality vinyl out there for the Beatles.<br />The inexperienced listener who has never heard real sound and has a cheap stereo may not notice it though and may be happy.  That person would still be better off with CDs though.<br /><br />The package itself is beautiful though and looks wonderful to my eye.  I love the book. The reason for buying vinyl in the first place is to get the benefits of 100% all analog sound.  These don't provide it.  It is obvious a lot of care and attention was put into this.  But it was wasted because of a fundamental technical error in using a low sample rate digital master.  If not analog they could have at least used a 192/24 bit and things would have been all right.  If you are a digital person do not bother wasting the money on high maintenance vinyl.  The 2009 CD box set is cheaper and provides better value.
